╭────────────────────────────────────────────pastedApr 12This is a genuinely important finding and it changes the conversation. Let me walk through what it means carefully, because there are two very different interpretations and only one of them is correct.
The Numbers In Plain Words

1,501 dropped touches had a mean NetScore of 12.79. Max of any dropped touch: 39.
111 traded touches had a mean NetScore of 85.0. All 111 scored 60+.
There is a complete gap between dropped and traded scores: 0 dropped touches scored 40+, 0 traded touches scored under 60.

The filter is not a cliff killing marginal signals. It's a wall separating two completely disjoint populations. The dropped touches aren't failed versions of the trades — they're categorically different signals that ConfluenceEngine judges as garbage.
Plus one ugly fact: Layer C (MTF context) contributed 0.00 to every single dropped touch. That's not a small number. That's zero across 1,501 rows. Either Layer C is broken, or Layer C's signal genuinely never lights up on any of these touches, or MTF context is gated in a way we don't understand.
Two Ways To Read This, One Is Right
Reading 1 (Optimistic): "The Floor Is Working Great"
Look at the gap — traded touches averaged 85, dropped touches averaged 13. The filter is separating the wheat from the chaff beautifully. We don't need to touch the floor, we don't need to rethink the filter, we can just accept the 93% drop rate as the cost of quality control.
This reading is wrong. Here's why.
Reading 2 (Skeptical): "The Score Is Not Actual Edge"
A high ConfluenceEngine score is a score. It's not a trade outcome. We've demonstrated that ConfluenceEngine consistently scores some touches high and others low. What we haven't demonstrated is that those high-scoring touches actually perform better than the low-scoring ones would have.
Remember the distinction:

ConfluenceEngine's job: assign a number to every touch based on a rulebook
What we care about: does that number predict PnL

The 12.79 vs 85 gap proves ConfluenceEngine is opinionated. It proves nothing about whether ConfluenceEngine's opinions are correct. The 111 traded touches produced 43.2% WR with AvgPnL around breakeven. That's the result of trading only the signals ConfluenceEngine thinks are great. If ConfluenceEngine's scoring reflected real edge, a score-85 average should produce dramatically better than breakeven. It doesn't.
That's the sobering implication. A scoring system that puts 1,501 touches below 40 and 111 touches above 60, with zero overlap, is either:
(a) Correctly identifying the 7% that have real edge — in which case the 111 traded touches should perform very well (they don't, they're breakeven), OR
(b) Sorting touches by a rulebook that doesn't correlate well with actual outcomes — in which case the floor is arbitrary and the dropped touches might contain just as much edge as the traded ones.
Our 43.2% breakeven WR from the traded subset strongly suggests we're in case (b). The filter is separating touches into two piles, but the "good" pile isn't performing like it should if the scoring were calibrated.
The Thing That Can't Be Ignored: Layer C = 0
Layer C (MTF) contributed 0 to every single dropped touch. That's an anomaly, not a feature of the data. Either:

Layer C is not running
Layer C is running but something is zeroing its contribution
Layer C is gated on a condition that's never met on touch bars
There's a bug silently swallowing Layer C's output

This matters because if Layer C is meant to add MTF context and it's not contributing anything, the scoring system is operating on three layers instead of four. The floor was presumably calibrated assuming four layers. If one is missing, the floor is wrong.
This needs investigating before any other decision.
Forward-Return Is Now Even More Important
Without forward-return data, we have a system where:

93% of touches are dropped because ConfluenceEngine scored them low
The 7% that trade are breakeven
We can't tell if the 93% would have been winners, losers, or the same as the 7%

Forward-return is the only way to answer: does ConfluenceEngine's scoring actually correlate with edge? If it does, dropped touches had worse forward returns than traded touches, on average. If it doesn't, the filter is arbitrary selection.
This makes forward-return not just "useful infrastructure" — it's the single thing that determines whether the entire scoring/floor architecture is validated or invalidated.
Revised Recommendation
Two parallel tracks, both urgent.
Track 1 (Diagnostic): Why Is Layer C Zero?
Quick investigation. Read-only. Look at how Layer C is computed in ConfluenceEngine, check what SnapKeys it depends on, verify those SnapKeys are publishing on touch bars. This is a ~30-minute analysis, no code changes.
If Layer C is broken, fix it. Re-run backtest. Scores will change. Filter behavior will change. Everything in our analysis might change.
Track 2 (Strategic): Build Forward-Return Logging
The original Chunk 3-5 plan. Now with clearer motivation. Once we have forward-return data on both traded AND dropped touches, we can directly measure:

Do high-score touches (60+) have better forward returns than low-score touches (0-39)?
Is the score-85 mean of traded touches actually associated with better outcomes than the score-13 mean of dropped touches?
Where does the "true" floor actually sit in terms of forward-return-positive vs negative?

This gives us the first evidence-based answer to whether the floor is doing useful work.
What I'm Holding Back On
Path A (hard filter on TRD=+1 shorts) is on indefinite hold. With the new evidence that ConfluenceEngine scoring may not correlate with edge, adding another filter on top would be layering guesses on unproven machinery. We need to validate the underlying filter before we add more filtering.
Decision
Three questions for you:

Layer C investigation first? I'd do that before anything else — it's cheap, and if broken, fixing it invalidates all subsequent analysis. If Layer C is intentionally-zero-by-design (e.g., only lights up on specific SMF setups), we learn that and move on quickly. 30 minutes worth of investigation.
After Layer C investigation, commit to forward-return? This is the bigger build. Once done, every future "does X feature predict PnL" question is answerable. Does not depend on Layer C outcome.
Accept that Path A is on hold? No filter changes until forward-return gives us evidence one way or another.

My strong recommendation: yes to all three, in that order. Layer C investigation → forward-return build → then revisit all scoring/filtering decisions with real counterfactual data.

So the actual chain for ORB signals is:

ConfluenceEngine Layer C: empty when hasVol=true → contributes 0
FootprintEntryAdvisor: explicitly bypassed for ORB\_\* signals
Result: ORB signals have zero order-flow contribution to scoring whatsoever, despite Volumetric data being available

This is a logic bug created by an architectural decision. Comment #28 says "Layer C scoring is now handled EXCLUSIVELY by the FootprintEntryAdvisor to avoid double-counting." But ORB explicitly bypasses the Advisor. So nothing handles Layer C for ORB. The score component is silently nuked for the only condition set that's currently firing.
What This Means For Everything
Every analytic conclusion from this session is now suspect to a degree. Specifically:
The 1,501 dropped touches with mean Net=12.79? Their actual Net would be higher — possibly significantly higher — if Layer C were computing for ORB. Meaning the floor wasn't filtering as aggressively as we thought, OR the 12.79 mean is missing a real component that would have pushed many of them above the floor.
The 111 traded touches with mean Net=85? Same — these scores are also missing the Layer C contribution. The "85 vs 13" gap is real but it's a gap measured on incomplete scores. Whether the gap survives with proper Layer C is unknown.
The TRD=+1 short bucket with -$53.93 PnL? That data is real (the trades happened, the PnL is what it is) — but our framing of "ConfluenceEngine should have caught these" is wrong because ConfluenceEngine's Layer C wasn't producing output for these.
The breakeven 43.2% WR? That's the result of trading signals scored by a 3-of-4-layer scoring system, not the 4-layer system the floor was calibrated against.
The Decision Point
Two options for fixing this:
Option A — Quick fix: Remove the volumetric bypass. Change the if (hasVol) block to fall through to the same scoring logic the non-volumetric path uses. Layer C starts producing scores again. Re-run backtest. See what changes.
Pro: Cheap. Just removes the empty branch.
Con: The original FIX #28 comment says this was done "to avoid double-counting" — meaning whoever made this change believed Layer C and Footprint scoring would double-count if both ran. Removing the bypass might bring back that double-counting. But since the FootprintEntryAdvisor bypass for ORB means double-counting wasn't happening for ORB anyway, this is probably safe for ORB but risky for other future condition sets.
Option B — Proper fix: Make Layer C compute when hasVol=true regardless of FootprintEntryAdvisor running. Add the same bd / regime / VWAP / h1Rising scoring inside the if (hasVol) branch. This makes Layer C scoring work for all condition sets independent of FootprintEntryAdvisor.
Pro: Surgical. ORB and any future bypassed condition sets get Layer C scoring. FootprintEntryAdvisor still handles its own scoring on top.
Con: Re-introduces the double-counting concern that motivated FIX #28. We'd need to verify whether FootprintEntryAdvisor's scoring overlaps with these specific Layer C signals. Quick read of the code says the Advisor is more about veto logic than score addition, so probably safe — but worth verifying.
Option C — Defer fix, instrument first. Don't fix Layer C yet. Build forward-return logging first as planned. Then once we have forward-return data, we'll see whether dropped vs traded touches actually had different forward returns. If they did, Layer C might not matter much. If they didn't, Layer C is one of multiple things to revisit.
My Honest Read
This is a meaningful bug. It shouldn't be deferred. But fixing it requires care because we don't know the full consequences of FIX #28's reasoning.
My recommendation: Option B (proper fix), but verify the double-counting concern first by reading FootprintEntryAdvisor's actual contribution to scoring.
If FootprintEntryAdvisor turns out to add scoring (not just veto), we need a more careful fix that conditions Layer C on whether FootprintEntryAdvisor actually ran for this candidate. If FootprintEntryAdvisor only vetoes, Option A is safe.
This investigation is still cheap — one more read-only Gemini run.

Q1 — FootprintEntryAdvisor.Evaluate() and Decision Structure

- Evaluate Method: (Lines 447–495) Calculates an additive score from five components: DirectionalPressure, ExtremeDefense, Absorption, StackedImbalance, and CumDeltaAgreement. It also checks for hard vetoes.
- FootprintEntryDecision: A readonly struct containing:
  _ Action: Deny, ApproveWeak, or ApproveStrong.
  _ IsVetoed: bool (used to kill candidates).
  _ SupportScore: int (the calculated additive score).
  _ Multiplier: double (0.90 for Weak, 1.10 for Strong by default). \* Reason: string for logging.
  Q2 — Usage in HostStrategy.ApplyFootprintEntryAdvisor
- Bypass Rule: (Lines 497–501) Explicitly bypasses the advisor for any candidate starting with "ORB\_".
- Score Impact: (Line 505) For non-ORB candidates, the advisor multiplies the existing RawScore by ea.Multiplier.
- Missing Logic: The advisor's SupportScore is completely ignored. It is never added to the candidate's score.
  Q3 — FootprintEntryAdvisorConfig Thresholds
- Default Settings: (Lines 328–356)
  _ DenyBelowScore: 35
  _ StrongApproveAtOrAboveScore: 65
  _ WeakApproveMultiplier: 0.90

  The Full Bug Picture
  There are actually two distinct problems that compound:
  Problem 1: Layer C Empty For All Volumetric Candidates
  ConfluenceEngine line 133: when hasVol=true, Layer C is an empty block. Should contribute up to +30. Contributes 0.
  Problem 2: FootprintEntryAdvisor's SupportScore Is Discarded
  This is the bigger surprise. The Advisor computes a SupportScore from five components (DirectionalPressure, ExtremeDefense, Absorption, StackedImbalance, CumDeltaAgreement). Then HostStrategy line 505 takes only the Multiplier (0.90, 1.00, or 1.10) and throws away the SupportScore entirely.
  So the comment at FIX #28 — "Layer C scoring is now handled EXCLUSIVELY by FootprintEntryAdvisor" — is categorically false for everyone, not just ORB. The Advisor never adds to score. It only multiplies by ±10%. Whoever wrote FIX #28 either misunderstood how the Advisor's output was being consumed, or someone later changed the consumption pattern without re-examining the comment.

  Condition SetLayer C contributionAdvisor contributionORB\__ (volumetric)0 (empty branch)Bypassed entirelyNon-ORB (volumetric)0 (empty branch)Multiplier only (0.90–1.10)Any condition set (non-volumetric)up to ~22 (proxies)Multiplier only (0.90–1.10)
  The intended Layer C contribution of up to +30 points is missing across the entire codebase whenever volumetric data is on. Which is essentially always.
  The Decision Has Become Larger
  This isn't just "fix the ORB Layer C bypass." This is "the entire scoring system is operating with a missing layer." Implications:

The 60-point floor was never really a 60-point floor — it's been functioning as roughly a 30-40 point floor in real terms because Layer C was always going to add 0
The 85-point mean of traded touches was achieved without Layer C — meaning if Layer C is fixed and starts contributing, the means and the floor relationship will shift
FootprintEntryAdvisor's SupportScore (which someone designed and wrote) is dead code from a scoring perspective — only its Multiplier and Veto outputs flow through

Three Possible Fixes Now
This is no longer a "quick fix" decision. Let me lay out three options clearly.
Option 1 — Minimal: Drop The hasVol Bypass, Use The Fallback Path
Single change in ConfluenceEngine: remove the empty if (hasVol) block, let everyone fall through to the existing fallback proxies (BarDelta, Regime, VWAP, H1Rising). All condition sets (ORB and non-ORB) get the same proxies-based Layer C scoring whether volumetric is on or off.
Pro: Tiny change. ConfluenceEngine logic becomes simpler. ORB starts getting Layer C points.
Con: We're leaving Volumetric data unused at the ConfluenceEngine level. The richer footprint data that's available when hasVol=true doesn't contribute to scoring beyond what the proxies already compute.
Option 2 — Proper: Fix Layer C To Use Volumetric Data When Available, Plus Wire Advisor's SupportScore
Two changes:

ConfluenceEngine: when hasVol=true, compute Layer C from the actual volumetric data (BarDelta, AbsorptionScore, StackedImbalanceBull/Bear, CumDelta, etc. — all SnapKeys we already publish)
HostStrategy: actually USE ea.SupportScore from the Advisor — add it to the candidate's RawScore, not just multiply by the Multiplier

Pro: All the score components someone designed actually contribute. Volumetric data finally pays off in ConfluenceEngine. SupportScore stops being dead code.
Con: Bigger change. Now we have to think about double-counting risk for non-ORB signals (Layer C using BarDelta + Advisor using BarDelta = double-counting on that input). Need to design this carefully.
Option 3 — Deferred: Don't Fix Anything Now, Build Forward-Return First
Accept that the scoring is broken. Build forward-return logging on top of the current broken scoring. Use forward-return data to learn whether the broken scoring was incidentally producing decent results. Then decide whether to fix Layer C.
Pro: Forces us to gather evidence before changing anything. Aligns with "measure before changing" discipline.
Con: Builds infrastructure on broken foundation. Forward-return data on broken scoring tells us what broken scoring does, not what fixed scoring would do.
What I Actually Recommend
Option 1, then forward-return.
Reasoning, not romance:

Option 1 is the safest correction. The existing fallback path was written and presumably tested. We're using it for hasVol=false cases anyway. Extending it to hasVol=true cases unifies the code paths.
Option 2 is more "correct" but introduces real overfitting risk. Wiring SupportScore into the score calculation, redesigning Layer C to use volumetric data — these are designed-from-scratch changes. We'd be picking values for new score weights without measurement. Document 2 was specifically against this.
Option 3 ignores a known bug. We just discovered Layer C contributes 0. Building elaborate measurement infrastructure on top of acknowledged-broken scoring is poor engineering.

The Option 1 fix is small, conservative, and gets us to a state where the scoring system works as the fallback path was designed. Then we run the same backtest, see how it changes, and then build forward-return on the corrected foundation.
We should be very honest about what Option 1 does and doesn't do:

✅ Fixes the bug where ORB candidates have no Layer C contribution
✅ Unifies the volumetric and non-volumetric code paths
✅ Doesn't introduce any new design decisions
❌ Doesn't make use of the volumetric data's richer information
❌ Doesn't fix the dead-code problem with Advisor's SupportScore

Both ❌s are deferred to a later phase, not solved now. After we have forward-return data, if the analysis shows volumetric-data-aware Layer C scoring would help, we make that change with evidence. Same for SupportScore wiring.

What Option 2 Actually Requires
Two changes, but each has a design question we have to answer.
Change 1: Layer C With Volumetric Data
When hasVol=true, what should Layer C compute?
The fallback path (when hasVol=false) uses these signals: BarDelta direction, Regime alignment, VWAP side, H1Rising. These produce up to ~22 points.
When hasVol=true, we have richer data available:

BarDelta (also available without volumetric — same SnapKey)
AbsorptionScore (volumetric only)
StackedImbalanceBull/Bear (volumetric only)
CumDelta direction (volumetric)
DeltaExhaustion flag (volumetric)
BullDivergence/BearDivergence flags (volumetric, computed by FootprintDivergenceTracker)
ImbalZoneAtBull/Bear (volumetric)

The ORIGINAL Layer C constants in the file (lines 47-51) reference these:
LAYER_C_DIVERGENCE = 15 // CVD divergence confirms
LAYER_C_DELTA_SL = 8 // buying at bar low
LAYER_C_ABS_MAX = 7 // absorption (graduated)
LAYER_C_DELTA_EXHST = 8 // delta exhaustion confirms
LAYER_C_IMBAL_ZONE = 6 // price at historical imbalance zone
These constants exist in the file but are unused anywhere in the code now (presumably they were used before FIX #28 emptied the block). They sum to 44, capped at 30. Same cap as before.
Design question A: Do we resurrect these original weights as-is, or pick different ones?
My answer: resurrect as-is. Reasoning — they were presumably tested and considered reasonable before someone deleted the code. We're not introducing new weights, we're un-deleting old ones. If they turn out to be wrong, we measure and adjust later with forward-return data. Resurrecting existing weights is closer to "fixing a bug" than "adding new logic."
If we picked different weights, we'd be making a decision today without measurement. The existing constants represent some prior author's calibration, which at least anchors us to a starting point that wasn't picked five minutes ago.
Change 2: Wire SupportScore From The Advisor
The Advisor computes a SupportScore but only Multiplier is used. To wire SupportScore in, HostStrategy line 505 changes from:
csharpc.RawScore = (int)Math.Round(c.RawScore _ ea.Multiplier, MidpointRounding.AwayFromZero);
to something like:
csharpc.RawScore = (int)Math.Round((c.RawScore + ea.SupportScore) _ ea.Multiplier, MidpointRounding.AwayFromZero);
Design question B: SupportScore is added before or after the multiplier?
Adding before multiplier: (RawScore + SupportScore) _ Multiplier — Advisor's contribution gets amplified/dampened by the Advisor's own multiplier, which is weird because the Advisor is signing off on its own work.
Adding after multiplier: RawScore _ Multiplier + SupportScore — Multiplier only affects the original score, SupportScore is a clean additive contribution.
My answer: after the multiplier. The semantic intent is "the Advisor's confidence (Multiplier) speaks to whether the original signal is good, then the Advisor's own evidence (SupportScore) is a separate additive contribution." Don't let the Advisor multiply its own output.
Design question C: What's the magnitude of SupportScore? If it can be 0-100, adding it to a RawScore in the same range distorts everything. If it's small (0-15), it's an honest additive bonus.
I need Gemini to read the Advisor and tell me the actual range of SupportScore values before I commit to wiring it directly. If it's 0-30, that's reasonable. If it's 0-100, we need to scale it down or rethink.
The Double-Counting Concern (Original FIX #28)
The whole reason FIX #28 emptied Layer C was to avoid double-counting between Layer C scoring and Advisor scoring. Now that we know:

ORB bypasses Advisor entirely — no double-count risk for ORB
Advisor's SupportScore was being discarded — no double-count was happening even for non-ORB
We're about to wire SupportScore in, AND restore Layer C

We're potentially creating the double-counting that FIX #28 worried about for non-ORB candidates. Specifically: BarDelta affects both Layer C scoring AND Advisor's DirectionalPressure component.
Design question D: Do we accept the double-counting (BarDelta ends up in both layers), or skip the BarDelta portion of Layer C when Advisor is going to run?
My answer: Accept the double-counting for now. The overlap is one signal (BarDelta) out of many in each layer. It's a known imperfection but introducing conditional logic ("compute Layer C differently when Advisor is bypassed vs not bypassed") creates more complexity than it solves. After forward-return data arrives, we measure whether the double-counting is causing miscalibration.

The Honest Position
We have two genuinely separate problems and I conflated them.
Problem A: Untested scoring weights for Layer C and SupportScore. These exist in the codebase but have never been validated. They're as good as any other made-up numbers. We have no basis to choose them.
Problem B: A real bug where Layer C contributes 0 for all volumetric ORB signals. The intended Layer C contribution (whatever the right weights are) is missing.
Problem A is unsolvable with what we have. We need forward-return data to know which weights are right.
Problem B is solvable, but only through Option 1 (the conservative fix) or by deferring entirely.
Revised Recommendation

Specifically:

Doesn't introduce any untested weights
Uses code that already runs for the non-volumetric path, so it's not new logic
Brings the max possible Layer C score down from "the original 30 cap" to ~22, but every signal in the existing dataset was already getting 0, so this is strictly an improvement

Don't wire SupportScore. It was a max-115 number with weights nobody validated. Wiring it would make things less measurable, not more. Leave it as dead code for now. Note it as a known issue. Revisit when forward-return data shows whether the Advisor's components correlate with edge.
Don't resurrect the original Layer C constants. Same reason. They were picked by intuition. Use the existing fallback path's already-running logic instead.
After Option 1, the Layer C contribution is small (max ~22) and deterministic. We can measure its actual effect in the re-run. If the re-run shows the floor is now too easy to clear (lots more trades, no improvement in PnL), we know Layer C is adding noise. If trades become more selective and PnL improves, the fallback path's signals were doing useful work.
This is the conservative move. It's also the move that preserves your ability to reason about what changed.

Your call.So lets use whatever weights i had, for now lets connectes it properly once we have the forward-return then we can evaluate the scores with proper data.Apr 13Stop. I want to make sure I'm hearing you correctly because this is a meaningful reversal of what we just agreed.
You're saying: wire up everything as it was originally designed (Layer C with the original 15/8/7/8/6 weights, AND SupportScore added to the score chain), then use forward-return data to evaluate whether those weights are right.
Before I write any prompt, I need to push back honestly because this contradicts the discipline document 2 warned us about. Let me lay out what I think the trade-off is so you can confirm or override with full information.
What This Approach Means
You'd be putting untested weights into the production scoring path, then using forward-return to measure them after the fact. The reasoning is reasonable on its face: "we need these weights connected to measure them at all."
But there's a subtle problem with that logic. You don't actually need them in the scoring path to measure them. You can log all the component values per touch (Layer C raw signals, Advisor's SupportScore breakdown) without those values affecting which signals get traded. Then forward-return tells you which components correlate with edge, and you wire only the proven ones in afterward.
Doing it the other way (wire first, measure second) means:

Every trade decision for the next backtest run is being made with weights you've explicitly acknowledged are guesses
The forward-return data you collect will reflect outcomes shaped by those guesses
You can't cleanly tell whether a feature has edge "in absolute terms" vs "given the existing weighted system that selected it"

The Risk I'm Worried About
The dataset becomes self-referential. Example: if Layer C weights heavily favor BarDelta (+7 for directional bar pressure), then every trade that gets selected has positive BarDelta. When forward-return data comes back, BarDelta will look like a great predictor — but only because it was already in the selection criteria. We won't know if it actually predicts edge or just predicts "this signal was selected."
To measure the weights properly, the touches that get scored need to span a wide enough range that we can see WHAT correlates with outcomes. If the floor cuts off everything below a certain score and the score is dominated by the new untested weights, the surviving population becomes narrow and biased.
Two Alternative Paths
Path X (your proposal): Wire both fixes now, evaluate later. Risk: self-referential dataset.
Path Y (what I'd recommend): Wire Layer C only with the conservative fallback path (Option 1), DO NOT wire SupportScore, but log SupportScore and all Layer C components per touch so forward-return data can evaluate them. This way forward-return measures what untested weights would have done, without their contamination of the trade selection. After we see the data, we wire what works.
Path Z (compromise): Wire both fixes for proper integration as you said. BUT also log the per-component values for both Layer C and SupportScore separately at touch time. This way we have both: the system runs end-to-end as designed, AND we have raw data to evaluate the weights independently. Slightly redundant logging, but it lets us answer "would different weights have been better" rather than just "did these weights produce edge."
My Honest Read
If you want the system to function the way you originally designed it before measurement-driven tuning, that's defensible — it's the working baseline you want to evaluate against. But I want to make sure you understand that the forward-return data you collect after this change won't cleanly tell you "are the weights right." It'll tell you "given these weights in the selection chain, here's what happened."
The cleanest answer for measurement is Path Y. The cleanest answer for "make the system work as designed" is Path X. The cleanest answer for "do both" is Path Z.
What I Need You To Confirm
Pick one:
Path X — Wire Layer C with original weights AND SupportScore additively. Accept that forward-return data will reflect this scoring. I'll write the prompt now.
Path Y — Wire only the conservative Layer C fix (Option 1, fallback path). Add per-touch logging of Layer C original weights AND SupportScore as separate fields so forward-return can evaluate them without them being in the selection chain. Slightly more work but cleaner science.
Path Z — Wire both fixes properly (your original proposal) AND add per-component logging on top. Most code, most complete data, lets us A/B-style compare.
The right answer depends on what you value more: a working system as designed, or measurement cleanliness. Both are legitimate choices. I'd lean Y but it's your strategy.

```csharp
            // =============================================================
            // LAYER C — OrderFlow conviction (0–30)
            // =============================================================
            bool hasVol = snap.GetFlag(SnapKeys.HasVolumetric);

            if (hasVol)
            {
                // ── LAYER C: Footprint Centralization ──────────────────────────
                // FIX (#28): Layer C footprint scoring is now handled EXCLUSIVELY
                // by the FootprintEntryAdvisor to avoid double-counting.
                // This block is now a passthrough for volumetric mode.
                // Fallback path below still uses proxies when Volumetric is off.
            }
            else
            {
                // These are structurally weaker signals but always present.
                // Max possible here is ~22 vs 30 with full Volumetric — reflects
                // reduced conviction when real order flow data is unavailable.

                // BarDelta from DataFeed (always populated, even without Volumetric)
                // Positive = buyers were net aggressive this bar, negative = sellers.
                double bd = snap.Get(SnapKeys.BarDelta);
                if (isLong  && bd > 0) layerC += 7;   // directional bar pressure
                if (!isLong && bd < 0) layerC += 7;

                // SMF Regime agreement — money flow confirms direction
                int regime = (int)snap.Get(SnapKeys.Regime);
                if ((isLong && regime > 0) || (!isLong && regime < 0)) layerC += 6;

                // VWAP side
                if (snap.VWAP > 0)
                {
                    bool aboveVwap = p.Close > snap.VWAP;
                    if ((isLong && aboveVwap) || (!isLong && !aboveVwap)) layerC += 5;
                }

                // Higher1 (15-min) bar direction
                if (snap.Higher1.Closes != null && snap.Higher1.Closes.Length >= 2)
                {
                    bool h1Rising = snap.Higher1.Close > snap.Higher1.Closes[1];
                    if ((isLong && h1Rising) || (!isLong && !h1Rising)) layerC += 4;
                }

                // SMF NonConfirmation veto
                if (isLong  && snap.GetFlag(SnapKeys.NonConfLong))  isVetoed = true;
                if (!isLong && snap.GetFlag(SnapKeys.NonConfShort)) isVetoed = true;
            }

            layerC = Math.Min(layerC, 30);  // hard cap applies to both paths
```

```csharp
            // =============================================================
            // LAYER C — OrderFlow conviction (0–22 cap)
            // =============================================================
            //
            // PREVIOUS BUG (FIX #28 was incorrect):
            // FIX #28 emptied the volumetric path under the assumption that
            // FootprintEntryAdvisor would take over Layer C scoring. In fact:
            //   1. Advisor's SupportScore output is discarded by HostStrategy
            //      (only its Multiplier is used)
            //   2. Advisor is bypassed entirely for ORB candidates
            // Result: ORB signals running with volumetric data had Layer C = 0.
            //
            // CONSERVATIVE FIX:
            // Use the existing fallback proxies for ALL candidates regardless
            // of hasVol. These signals are weaker than full footprint scoring
            // but they're tested-by-time fallback logic. Max possible: ~22 pts.
            //
            // The original Layer C constants (LAYER_C_DIVERGENCE=15, etc.,
            // lines 47-51) remain defined but unused — they were untested
            // weights and we won't wire them until forward-return data

            double bd = snap.Get(SnapKeys.BarDelta);
            if (isLong  && bd > 0) layerC += 7;   // directional bar pressure
            if (!isLong && bd < 0) layerC += 7;

            // SMF Regime agreement — money flow confirms direction
            int regime = (int)snap.Get(SnapKeys.Regime);
            if ((isLong && regime > 0) || (!isLong && regime < 0)) layerC += 6;

            // VWAP side — price above VWAP = buyers in control, below = sellers
            if (snap.VWAP > 0)
            {
                bool aboveVwap = p.Close > snap.VWAP;
                if ((isLong && aboveVwap) || (!isLong && !aboveVwap)) layerC += 5;
            }

            if (snap.Higher1.Closes != null && snap.Higher1.Closes.Length >= 2)
            {
                bool h1Rising = snap.Higher1.Close > snap.Higher1.Closes[1];
                if ((isLong && h1Rising) || (!isLong && !h1Rising)) layerC += 4;
            }

            // SMF NonConfirmation veto — when SMF explicitly flags flow disagrees
            if (isLong  && snap.GetFlag(SnapKeys.NonConfLong))  isVetoed = true;
            if (!isLong && snap.GetFlag(SnapKeys.NonConfShort)) isVetoed = true;

            layerC = Math.Min(layerC, 30);  // hard cap retained for safety,
                                            // though new max is ~22
```

So for average PnL to quadruple on the same trend-aligned-short bucket, the new signals being admitted must be dramatically better than the existing population.
Possibility 1: The new signals admitted by the relaxed floor are, in aggregate, dramatically higher-quality.
Possibility 2: The backtest window changed and this is a regime effect (a wildly bullish/bearish year I didn't have before).
Possibility 3: There's a data artifact — something in how PnL is calculated or joined is different.
Before I celebrate, I need to verify which of these is true. A 4.4x PnL jump in a measurement shouldn't happen from a scoring-layer change alone. Either the floor relaxation exposed us to a previously-filtered subset that happens to be great (possible, would need forward-return data to explain) OR the backtest parameters are different from the prior run.

Minor, but worth noting: the script had errors on CH_L=1 and CH_S=1 buckets where W and L both came back blank. The parser is likely failing to read zero-count buckets correctly. Doesn't affect the main analysis but should be fixed in the script eventually.

The Clean A/B Test Requires The Same Window
To isolate the Layer C fix's effect, the two runs need to cover the same calendar period. Right now we have:

Run A: 2025-04-17 → 2026-04-10 (12 months) with OLD ConfluenceEngine
Run B: 2025-06-17 → 2026-04-10 (10 months) with NEW ConfluenceEngine

The only defensible comparison is: re-run with the OLD ConfluenceEngine on the 2025-06-17 → 2026-04-10 window. Then we'd have:

Run A': 2025-06-17 → 2026-04-10 with OLD ConfluenceEngine
Run B: 2025-06-17 → 2026-04-10 with NEW ConfluenceEngine

Both covering identical dates, differing only in the fix. That's the A/B comparison we want.
But we can't easily do that because the code has been committed with the fix. We'd have to either:
Option 1: Revert the fix, re-backtest the 10-month window, save Log.csv as "Log_prefix.csv", re-apply the fix, and compare. Safe but painful (two more backtest runs, one compile cycle).
Option 2: Re-run the NEW fix on the full 12-month window (2025-04-17 → 2026-04-10). We'd compare:

Run A: 12 months, OLD code (what we have)
Run B': 12 months, NEW code (need to run)
Same window, clean comparison.

Option 2 is simpler — one additional backtest, no code changes. Just set the backtest date range correctly in NinjaTrader.

Forward-Return Build — Full Plan
Before I write any prompt, let me lay out the design so you can push back if anything seems wrong.
Design Decision 1: Two-Row Pairing (Option B From Earlier)
Each touch generates two rows in Log.csv:

TOUCH row — written immediately at touch detection (already exists)
TOUCH_OUTCOME row — written N bars later with forward-return data

Both share the same SignalId for joining. Append-only, no buffer-state-at-risk.
Design Decision 2: What Forward-Return Fields To Log
At TOUCH_OUTCOME time, log for the same SignalId:

MFE (Max Favorable Excursion): farthest price moved in the intended direction, in dollars (tick-value adjusted)
MAE (Max Adverse Excursion): farthest against, in dollars
HitStop: 1 if price ever touched the proposed stop price during the window
HitTarget: 1 if price ever touched the proposed target (T1) during the window
FirstHit: "STOP" if stop hit first, "TARGET" if target hit first, "NEITHER" if window expired, "BOTH_SAME_BAR" if ambiguous
SimPnL: simulated PnL assuming the trade took the first-hit exit, or closed at window-end price if neither hit
BarsToFirstHit: how many bars until whichever hit first
CloseAtWindowEnd: the close price N bars after touch (even if stop/target hit earlier)

Nine fields. Enough to reconstruct the trade's hypothetical life.
Design Decision 3: Window Length — 60 Bars, Adjustable
60 bars on NQ 5-min = 5 hours. Covers most trading sessions from entry to close. We can tune later.
Stored as a class-level configurable, so we can change it without rebuilding.
Design Decision 4: New Class — ForwardReturnTracker
Standalone class. Single responsibility. Register touches when they happen, update them per bar, emit TOUCH_OUTCOME when complete.
csharppublic sealed class ForwardReturnTracker
{
private readonly StrategyLogger \_log;
private readonly int \_windowBars;
private readonly Dictionary<string, ActiveTouch> \_active; // keyed by SignalId

    public ForwardReturnTracker(StrategyLogger log, int windowBars = 60)
    {
        _log = log; _windowBars = windowBars;
        _active = new Dictionary<string, ActiveTouch>();
    }

    public void Register(string signalId, SignalDirection dir, double entry,
                          double stop, double target, int barIndex, DateTime time)
    {
        // Store active touch for tracking
    }

    public void OnBar(IBarContext primary, int currentBar)
    {
        // For every active touch, update MFE/MAE/HitStop/HitTarget
        // If window expired or stop/target hit, call Finalize()
    }

    private void Finalize(ActiveTouch t, double closePrice, DateTime time)
    {
        // Emit TOUCH_OUTCOME row via _log
        _active.Remove(t.SignalId);
    }

}
Design Decision 5: Integration With Existing Code
Two touch points:
A. StrategyLogger.LogTouchEvent — extend signature to include stop/target prices. These aren't currently passed (we only pass zone bounds). The CallSite in ORB_Classic needs updating.
B. HostStrategy — instantiate ForwardReturnTracker and call:

Register(...) when ORB calls LogTouchEvent (or in LogTouchEvent itself if cleaner)
OnBar(...) every bar in OnBarUpdate's producer chain

C. StrategyLogger.LogTouchOutcome — new method that writes the TOUCH_OUTCOME row. Mirror pattern to LogTouchEvent.
Design Decision 6: Where Registration Happens
Two options:
Option 1: LogTouchEvent calls \_tracker.Register() internally. Single call site (ORB), cleaner.
Option 2: ORB calls both \_log.LogTouchEvent(...) AND \_log.Tracker.Register(...) separately. More explicit but redundant.
I prefer Option 1. Single responsibility: when you log a touch, you're registering for outcome tracking. The two are inseparable from a measurement perspective.
This means StrategyLogger needs a reference to ForwardReturnTracker, or the tracker needs to be injected into LogTouchEvent. Clean implementation: StrategyLogger holds a ForwardReturnTracker reference set after construction (logger.SetTracker(tracker)), so the dependency direction is clean.
Design Decision 7: Bar Data Source
The tracker needs access to High[0], Low[0], Close[0], Time[0] each bar to update MFE/MAE and detect stop/target hits. Two options:
Option 1: Tracker takes IBarContext in OnBar(...). HostStrategy passes the primary snapshot each bar.
Option 2: Tracker has a reference to the IDataFeed and queries it.
Option 1 is cleaner — the tracker is a pure consumer, not a data-feed owner.
Breakdown Into Manageable Chunks
Despite being "the big build," I want to break this into 3 prompt-sized chunks, each with its own verification. Failure recovery is easier if each chunk compiles on its own.
Chunk F-1: Extend LogTouchEvent signature to include stop/target, update ORB_Classic to pass them. Add a new LogTouchOutcome method to StrategyLogger. Compile and verify. No behavior change yet (nothing writes TOUCH_OUTCOME rows).
Chunk F-2: Build ForwardReturnTracker.cs as a standalone class. Wire it into HostStrategy (instantiate, pass to StrategyLogger, call OnBar each bar, Register from LogTouchEvent). Compile and verify.
Chunk F-3: Run a short 2-3 day backtest. Verify TOUCH_OUTCOME rows appear with real MFE/MAE/SimPnL values. Update the analysis script to parse them. If all three chunks clear, run the full 10-month backtest.
This is 3 compile cycles. Higher than our typical chunk but each one is logically cohesive.
Questions Before I Write Chunk F-1
Three decisions I want you to confirm before writing any code:

Window length: 60 bars (5 hours). Good, or do you want different? ORB reload trades typically resolve faster — 30 bars might be more appropriate. But longer is safer (covers slow-moving trades). Pick:

A: 60 bars default
B: 30 bars default
C: Different number

Should the tracker track trades that DID become real trades, or skip them? Real trades already have actual PnL in Trades.csv. Running the simulation on them gives us "simulated vs actual" calibration. Worth doing? Options:

A: Track ALL touches (traded and dropped) — lets us validate simulation accuracy
B: Only track DROPPED touches — lighter weight, cleaner logic

Exit rule for simulation: When neither stop nor target hit within window, what's the "exit"? Options:

A: Close at bar N+60 (window-end) — simple, standard
B: Close at "end of session" or N+60, whichever comes first — more realistic for intraday
C: Keep tracking until stop/target or EOD, then close at EOD

My picks: A (60 bars), A (track all touches for calibration), B (session-aware window).

Locked in:

Window: 60 bars (default, adjustable)
Track all touches, traded and dropped (calibration + comprehensive visibility)
Session-aware window: 60 bars OR session close, whichever comes first
Strategy-agnostic: ForwardReturnTracker works for any condition set. ORB is the first consumer, but BOSWave, FVG_Retest, custom future strategies all get it free.

Before writing the prompt, let me think about strategy-agnosticism explicitly so the architecture is right the first time.
What "Strategy-Agnostic" Requires

ForwardReturnTracker has no knowledge of ORB. It takes generic parameters: signalId, direction, entry, stop, target, bar, time. It doesn't care which condition set produced them.
LogTouchEvent is already strategy-agnostic (any condition set calls it with its own SignalId and zoneType). We just extend its signature to include stop/target.
Registration happens inside LogTouchEvent itself, not in ORB. Any condition set that logs a touch gets automatic forward-return tracking — no per-strategy wiring required.
The CSV row format is generic. TOUCH_OUTCOME rows share a common schema. Any future condition set's outcomes land in the same rows, analyzable by the same scripts.
Registration is conditional on having a valid stop/target. If a condition set doesn't have those (e.g., a strategy that exits on a signal-flip rather than price levels), it can pass 0/0 and the tracker handles it gracefully.

For ORB_Measure and other future condition sets — I'm not sure. CHANGE 2 — Add LogTouchOutcome method to StrategyLogger.cs
========================================================================

Add immediately AFTER the LogTouchEvent method (before EvalNoSignal).

```csharp
        // =========================================================================
        // TOUCH OUTCOME LOG (forward-return data)
        // =========================================================================
        //
        // Tag = TOUCH_OUTCOME. Written by ForwardReturnTracker N bars after a
        // TOUCH event. Contains simulated forward-return data: MFE, MAE, whether
        // stop/target would have been hit, hypothetical PnL, etc.
        //
        // Joins to TOUCH rows via SignalId. Strategy-agnostic — any condition
        // set's touches get tracked automatically once registered with the
        // ForwardReturnTracker.
        // =========================================================================

        /// <summary>
        /// Log the forward-return outcome of a touch event N bars after it occurred.
        /// Joinable to the original TOUCH row via signalId.
        /// </summary>
        public void LogTouchOutcome(
            string signalId,
            string conditionSetId,
            SignalDirection direction,
            double mfeDollars,          // max favorable excursion
            double maeDollars,          // max adverse excursion
            int hitStop,                // 1 if stop would have been hit
            int hitTarget,              // 1 if target would have been hit
            string firstHit,            // "STOP" | "TARGET" | "NEITHER" | "BOTH_SAMEBAR"
            double simPnL,              // simulated PnL (dollars)
            int barsToFirstHit,         // bars until whichever hit first, 0 if neither
            double closeAtWindowEnd,    // close price at end of simulation window
            int windowBars,             // actual bars tracked (may be < default if session closed)
            DateTime outcomeTime)       // bar time when outcome was finalized
        {
            if (!WriteCsv || _writer == null) return;

            string detail = string.Format(
                "MFE={0:F2} MAE={1:F2} HIT_STOP={2} HIT_TARGET={3} " +
                "FIRST_HIT={4} SIM_PNL={5:F2} BARS_TO_HIT={6} " +
                "CLOSE_END={7:F2} WINDOW_BARS={8}",
                mfeDollars, maeDollars, hitStop, hitTarget,
                firstHit, simPnL, barsToFirstHit,
                closeAtWindowEnd, windowBars);

            string dirStr = direction == SignalDirection.Long ? "L" : "S";

            WriteCsvRow(outcomeTime, "TOUCH_OUTCOME", CurrentBar,
                dirStr, "", conditionSetId,
                0, "", 0,
                simPnL, 0, 0, 0, 0, 0,
                0, 0, 0,
                signalId, firstHit, detail);
        }

```

Why This Happened
Gemini used WriteFile (full file replacement) instead of str_replace (surgical edit). The prompt didn't prevent it from doing this. When Gemini doesn't have the existing file fully in its context and uses WriteFile, it generates what it thinks the file should look like, not what minimal changes to apply.

Recovery Options
Option 1: Git Revert (Cleanest)

Option 2: Manual Surgical Reconstruction

Option 3: Partial Salvage
The LogTouchEvent extension and LogTouchOutcome additions themselves look correct in the current file. If we can restore everything ELSE back to working state while keeping those two changes, we'd preserve our progress. This is a hybrid of 1 and 2.
